Argentina Drops 1.6%
On Central-Bank Spat
Stocks in Argentina declined as investors were spooked by a dispute over the future of Central Bank
governor Martin Redrado.
Markets elsewhere in the Americas, and in Europe and Asia, were mostly higher.
Asian markets traded in tight ranges early Thursday, though consumer-related stocks surged in Sydney on
retail sales data. Japan was up 0.2% and New Zealand gained 0.3%; South Korea was down 0.1% and
Australia slipped 0.1%.
Mr. Redrado clung to power after President Cristina Fernandez on Wednesday asked him to resign,
frustrated over delays in transferring some $6.6 billion of the central bank's $48 billion foreign exchange
reserves to the Treasury to be able to make debt payments.
The Merval stock index declined 1.6% to 2362.46.

iShares Boosts Lead In the ETF Market --- BlackRock Brand Holds 50.1% of Assets
Fund Track
iShares Boosts Lead In the ETF Market --- BlackRock Brand Holds 50.1% of Assets
By Ian Salisbury
680 words
5 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Even for the ETF industry's dominant player, maintaining a big lead on rivals isn't easy when the field is
growing. But that is exactly what iShares did in 2009.
This exchange-traded-fund brand -- which was sold to BlackRock Inc. by Barclays PLC last year -- not only
maintained but also increased its market share in the ETF business in 2009, the first time it has done so since
2006.
At year-end, iShares ETFs held about $373 billion, or about 50.1% of the assets in all U.S. ETFs, according
to fund researcher Morningstar Inc. In 2008, iShares had 47.7% of ETF assets in 2008.
The gain, while relatively small, is impressive considering how intense competition is getting. Companies like
State Street Corp. and Vanguard Group have been trying hard to grab investors' ETF dollars with low-cost
funds and big marketing campaigns. Two other well-known firms, Charles Schwab Corp. and Pacific
Investment Management Co., a unit of Allianz SE, got into the ETF business in 2009. T. Rowe Price Group
Inc. and Goldman Sachs Group Inc. seem likely to join in coming months.
The overall number of ETFs competing for attention has swelled to almost 800 from about 150 five years ago,
according the Investment Company Institute, the fund industry's trade group. (ETFs are baskets of securities
that are traded on exchanges daily.)
In this context, iShares' dominance is "truly remarkable," says Matt Hougan, editor of IndexUniverse.com, a
Web site for passive investors.
Certainly, iShares enjoys some natural advantages. Nearly all ETFs are index funds, passively managed
investments that mirror returns of a benchmark. So it can be difficult for new products to differentiate
themselves.
Also, a momentum factor is at work here. Since investors trade ETFs on the market like a stock, they like to
buy established funds with heavy trading volume -- thus they know they will always be able to sell quickly and
cheaply. That puts new players at a disadvantage.
As a result, Mr. Hougan thinks iShares faces its biggest threat from Vanguard, the Malvern, Pa., company
known for popularizing index funds decades ago.
Vanguard didn't launch its first ETF until years after iShares, and the $92 billion in its ETFs still represents a
fraction of what the iShares funds hold. But low investment fees and Vanguard's famous brand name mean it
has been able to increase its ETF roster quickly in the past several years, building off of a small base.
State Street, an ETF pioneer, has seen its market share shrink steadily over the past decade, even as a small
handful of its funds, notably the Standard &Poor's 500-tracking SPDR (trading symbol: SPY), remain wildly
popular.
In 2009, iShares benefited especially from a strong lineup of fixed-income funds, one of the fastest growing
areas of the ETF business, where assets more than doubled since November 2008.
In 2002, iShares launched the first bond ETF, five years before its major competitors, and holds roughly $8
out of every $10 in these funds.
Investors' renewed interest in developing-country stocks this year also benefited iShares. Its $39 billion
emerging-markets fund is the third largest ETF on the market.

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Success in those two areas helped offset lost ground in one place iShares didn't prove a winner in 2009. Its
gold ETF took in about $450 million in new money from investors through November.
That isn't bad, but the total was meager compared to the $14 billion investors poured into a competing fund,
SPDR Gold Shares (GLD).
The dynamic highlights the importance to ETF companies of offering a broad array of funds to investors, says
Standard &Poor's ETF analyst Tom Graves.
Long the largest ETF family, iShares has nearly 190 different funds, targeting assets from oil- and
gas-exploration stocks to Treasury inflation-protected securities. Diversification "helps them out, if they're not
strong in one hot area" at any given moment, Mr. Graves says.

Page 146 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
New Year Brings Rally As Dow Jumps 155.91
Monday's Markets
New Year Brings Rally As Dow Jumps 155.91
By Peter A. McKay
466 words
5 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks began 2010 with the best performance in almost two months and commodity prices soared as
investors gained renewed faith in the global economic recovery.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average leapt 155.91 points, or 1.5%, to 10583.96, its biggest gain since Nov. 9
and its highest close in 15 months. Oil and copper jumped to levels unseen since 2008 and gold futures
climbed.
Manufacturing data released on Monday in China, Europe and the U.S. pointed to strengthening economies.
However, the prospects for 2010 are mixed. While the U.S. economy appears to be gradually recovering,
expectations for employment and consumer spending, key to corporate profits, are lackluster.
"You've got a lot of the economic data working in the right direction, plus there is a lot of money being put to
work to start the year," said strategist Bruce Bittles, of R.W. Baird &Co. "That said, I think this is going to be a
difficult market in which to make money in the longer term. Everything is in such a delicate state," following a
red-hot rally over the last three quarters of 2009.
Investors also bid overseas stocks higher, with hefty gains in some emerging markets, led by Brazilian
stocks, which closed at a 20-month high. Elsewhere, Britain's FTSE 100 rose 1.6%, Japan's Nikkei rose 1%,
and France's CAC 40 rose 2%.
As investors' risk appetite swelled, safe haven investments like the U.S. dollar dropped.
The U.S. Dollar Index, which measures the greenback against a basket of six other currencies, declined
0.4%. The benchmark 10-year Treasury note slipped 2/32 to yield 3.841%.
Traders and money managers said it will take a few more days for Wall Street's trading desks to return to full
strength and that the gradual return of participants may boost stocks and other assets in the near future.
January's performance is seen as a key barometer for the year -- a rise in the first month typically is followed
by a strong performance through the rest of the year.
The Nasdaq Composite gained 1.7%. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index climbed 1.6%.
The S&P's materials and energy categories led the gains as gold futures reached a two-week high and
crude-oil futures climbed above $81 a barrel.
The U.S. economy seems to be showing enough momentum that few investors fear a sudden dip back into
recession.
"The market is just giving no hint here of letting up," after 2009's gains, said Tom Alexander, president of
Alexander Trading. "If you're selling at this point, you're really just guessing, trying to pick a top."

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- Celebrating a Year Of Highs and Lows
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- Celebrating a Year Of Highs and Lows
13,186 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R10
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Wall Street lived, thanks in large part to Main Street. Hundreds of billions of dollars in taxpayer money helped
nurse the financial system back to relative health, with the country's biggest banks paying back the
government rescue aid by year's end. But executives' outsize compensation stood in contrast to an
unemployment rate that topped 10%.
Barack Obama was inaugurated president and set off on an ambitious agenda to remake health care, reform
the financial system and reclaim the U.S.'s standing on the world stage. The bear turned into a bull and the
Dow industrials reclaimed 10000 in October. Recession probably ended late in the year as global stimulus
efforts bore fruit. US Airways Capt. Chesley "Sully" Sullenberger was a hero with "the Miracle on the Hudson"
in a year of sex scandals, reality-TV stunts and financial schemes (alleged and proved) stealing headlines.
On the world stage, Iran and Afghanistan re-elected leaders under clouds of suspicion and violence.
Pyongyang tested nuclear weapons, while many countries suspect Tehran wants them. China flexed its
muscles on climate change, currency and a host of other issues as it assumed a more prominent role in
global affairs. Fiscal and debt burdens came to bear in Dubai and Greece. Terror still raged in Iraq,
Afghanistan and, in the year's waning days, in the air, as an alleged bomber tried to take down a Christmas
Day flight.
Here is a summary of news events from the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most cases, the dates
reflect when the items were published (not the date of the event itself).

MAY
1 Chrysler filed for bankruptcy protection. Chrysler's reorganization under Chapter 11 envisions the auto
maker being revitalized under a partnership between Italy's Fiat and the UAW.

Stocks around the world had a robust April, with some of the best advances in nearly two decades. The Dow
industrials were up 7.4% on the month. European stocks rose a best-ever 13.5%.
Supreme Court Justice David Souter plans to retire. The move by 69-year-old judge created the first vacancy
on the court for President Obama to fill.
2 Regulators shut down Silverton Bank in Atlanta. The FDIC, which couldn't find a buyer, estimated the failure
would cost its deposit insurance fund $1.3 billion.
Carol Ann Duffy became Britain's first female poet laureate in the job's 341-year history.

points, or 2.6%, to 8426.74, shaving the year-to-date loss to 3.9%.
6 An Ohio woman revealed she is the first U.S. recipient of a face transplant, performed at the Cleveland
Clinic.
7 At least seven of the biggest U.S. banks must boost their capital levels by a total of $65 billion, the
government's stress-tests found. J.P. Morgan Chase, Goldman, American Express and Capital One Financial
are among those whose levels were deemed sufficient. Wells Fargo was told it needs $13 billion to $15
billion. Bank of America was told a day earlier that it would need to address a $35 billion capital shortfall.

little sign of deficit-cutting.
China gave its first official tally of the number of schoolchildren killed in lthe May 12, 2008, quake, saying

Baseball suspended Manny Ramirez for 50 games for taking a banned substance.

12 Microsoft tapped long-term debt markets for the first time with a $3.75 billion offering, sparking speculation
about a big acquisition.
Lawyer Marc Dreier pleaded guilty to fraud and other charges in an alleged scheme to sell $700 million in
fake promissory notes and misuse client funds.
The Pentagon ousted its top general in Afghanistan, and replaced him with Lt. Gen. Stanley McChrystal, who
commanded the military's special operations forces in Iraq. The defense secretary also said he would name
Lt. Gen. David Rodriguez to run the day-to-day operations of all U.S. forces in Afghanistan, the first time the
U.S. will have two senior commanders in Kabul. The moves are part of a new strategy that relies more on
counterinsurgency tactics.
13 Freddie Mac posted a $9.85 billion loss and said it will need an additional $6.1 billion from the Treasury.
The government revised entitlement-fund estimates. The Medicare fund for hospital care will be depleted in
2017, two years earlier than previously estimated. Last year marked the first time Medicare paid out more in
benefits than funds it took in. The Social Security trust fund won't be exhausted until 2037.
14 The SEC staff is readying civil fraud charges against Countrywide Financial co-founder Angelo Mozilo, in
what would be the highest-profile government legal action against a CEO connected to the financial crisis.
The SEC filed civil fraud charges against Mr. Mozilo and two ex-lieutenants on June 4; all three denied
wrongdoing.
Lehman is moving to spin off its remaining assets, a collection of battered real-estate and private-equity
holdings valued at about $45 billion.
15 The Treasury will make federal bailout funds available to a number of life insurers, acting on the embattled
sector's long-running effort to get government help. The Treasury is prepared to inject as much as $22 billion
in TARP money into the firms. Many of the largest rejected the funds.
Scientists have dug up the fossil of a primate that could be the common ancestor of apes and humans.

20 The Senate approved, in a 90-5 vote, sweeping restrictions on credit-card companies that would ban extra
fees and fluctuating rates, and arm consumers with more information on their debts. The House passed the

Senate Democrats plan to block Guantanamo's closing. Bowing to political pressure, the lawmakers will
withhold funding to shut the prison until President Obama comes up with a detailed plan for handling its 241
detainees.

estimates will cost its weakened insurance fund $4.9 billion.
Xerox's Anne Mulcahy is stepping down as CEO after an eight-year run, and named her lieutenant, Ursula
Burns to the job. Ms. Burns became the first African-American woman to head a Fortune 500 company.
OpenTable's IPO rose 59% in the best debut since 2007.
President Obama and and former Vice President Dick Cheney clashed over terrorism policies. The president
declared that the nation had gone "off course" under President Bush by endorsing hard interrogation tactics
and offshore prisons. Minutes later, Mr. Cheney defended the policies as "legal, essential, justified,
successful and the right thing to do."
23 Morgan Stanley said it would raise base salaries of many top officers and earners, a move to cut reliance
on bonuses as the U.S. pushes pay-overhaul proposals.
North Korea's political transition may have begun. Kim Jong Il has initiated leadership changes in which his
brother-in-law and third son are emerging as central players in a new power structure, U.S. officials say. Days
later, the country set off its most powerful nuclear test. In early May, intelligence officials in Seoul said the
youngest son was tapped as the next leader.
27 Spain's Banco Santander, a Madoff "feeder," agreed to pay $235 million to settle potential legal claims by

President Obama tapped Sonia Sotomayor for high court. The appeals court judge would be the first Hispanic

The gay-marriage ban was upheld in California when the state's top court said it couldn't overturn the
voter-approved measure. The justices validated the 18,000 same-sex marriages that were performed in the
state before Proposition 8 was passed.
28 Pequot Capital is shutting its doors amid a revived insider-trading investigation.
Toys "R" Us will buy niche retailer FAO Schwarz, which will turn over operations but maintain its name.
29 Procter &Gamble will increase its offerings of lower-priced goods, a key shift from its reliance on

1 Abortion provider George Tiller was slain in Kansas. Dr. Tiller, 67, one of few doctors in the U.S. to perform

on both sides of the debate. The shooting followed a lull in anti-abortion violence after a mid-May appeal by
President Obama, at a commencement address at Notre Dame for common ground on the issue.
2 GM made its bankruptcy-court filing, the second-largest in history by an industrial company. The auto

4 A tape with the voice believed to be of Osama Bin Laden was released, saying Pakistan's fight against the

to the Israeli-Palestinian conflict, taking a tougher line on the Jewish state than his predecessor but also
noting a common enemy in extremism.
8 Roger Federer won the French Open, completing a career Grand Slam and tying Pete Sampras with 14

stake of up to 34% in the bank.
North Korea sentenced Euna Lee and Laura Ling, journalists for Current TV of San Francisco, to 12 years in

10 The Supreme Court cleared the way for Chrysler to exit from bankruptcy court, lifting a stay on its
proposed sale to a group including Fiat.
The first Guantanamo detainee arrived to face trial. The Tanzanian native appeared in federal court in New
York and pleaded not guilty to 286 counts related to the 1998 bombings of U.S. embassies in Kenya and
Tanzania and other terrorist activities allegedly carried out by al Qaeda.
11 A gunman opened fire at the U.S. Holocaust museum. A security guard was killed in the attack before the
alleged shooter was captured. Federal authorities identified the suspect as James von Brunn, an 88-year-old
man with a record of anti-Semitic statements and writings, notably on his Web site. Mr. Von Brunn was
charged with murder the next day.
The Obama administration scrapped the $500,000 salary cap it proposed for executives at firms receiving
large amounts of federal assistance, but it appointed a pay czar with broad authority.
12 CEO Kenneth Lewis defended Bank of America's Merrill deal in Washington as lawmakers questioned the
move and the role played by federal officials. A day prior, Fed officials' emails showed harsh criticism over
BofA's attempt to pull out of its Merrill deal.
BlackRock agreed to buy Barclays Global Investors from Barclays for $13.5 billion, a deal that will create a
money-management titan.with nearly $3 trillion in assets.
13 The Dow industrials pushed into positive territory for 2009, having gained 34% in three months. Financial
markets have been buoyed by the money being pumped out by governments around the world, and some
investors have begun speaking of a "bailout bubble" being created in certain markets.
Both candidates claimed victory in Iran's election. The official Iranian news agency declared President
Mahmoud Ahmadinejad the victor but his pro-reform rival Mir Hossein Mousavi alleged irregularities and
claimed he had won by a landslide. State media reported unprecedented turnout, and some polling stations
stayed open beyond their scheduled close. Over the next several days and weeks, Iranians took to the
streets in protest, with rallies intensifying after the government said Mr. Ahmadinejad had won with about
63% of the vote.
16 Extended Stay Hotels filed for Chapter 11 protection, collapsing under the debt from the firm's $8 billion

products, potentially spanning mortgages, credit cards, annuities and even basic bank accounts. The

were obtaining.

27 Emaar Properties may merge with units of Dubai Holding as a sharp drop in Middle East property values is

The House narrowly passed a major climate bill. The landmark legislation, approved 219-212, would curb
U.S. greenhouse-gas emissions, securing an initial victory for a key part of President Obama's agenda. By
putting a price on emissions, the bill would affect how much Americans pay to drive cars or to heat their
homes. It would mandate that 15% of the nation's electricity come from sources such as wind and solar
power by 2020.
29 The Honduran army exiled President Zelaya, ending his plan to redraw the constitution. The U.S. and

AUGUST
1 The U.S. economy came out of its tailspin in the second quarter and may be poised to resume growing, as

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- The Beaten-Down Rose in '09 --- Ford Was Year's Top Large-Cap Gainer; Investors Also Bet on...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- The Beaten-Down Rose in '09 --- Ford Was Year's Top
Large-Cap Gainer; Investors Also Bet on Tech, Commodities
By Matt Phillips
1,129 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As stocks rose from the rubble in 2009, investors reconsidered companies once thought to be on life support,
bet on commodities and technology and recalibrated their pessimism about once-popular shares beaten
down by the bleak sentiment and dire economics that characterized 2008.
Big winners in last year's comeback fall broadly into two categories, said Michael Sheldon, chief market
strategist at investment adviser RDM Financial. One can be thought of as economically sensitive stocks likely
to benefit from a global economic rebound, he said, "and second, companies . . . that have survived and are
now starting regain their footing."
Among large-capitalization stocks, companies that had at least $5 billion in market value at the start of 2009,
the highest flier was Ford Motor, which more than quadrupled to $10 in 2009, as the auto maker benefited
from its relatively stronger position than rivals General Motors and Chrysler. Of course, Ford's surge has to be
put in context. Its shares tumbled more than 60% in 2008, hitting a low of $1.26 on Nov. 19 of that year.
The basic-materials sector was one of the highflying slices of last year's market, as investors angled for
exposure to companies tied to commodities. Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold shares more than tripled as
the price of gold surged in response to a weak U.S. dollar during most of the year.
The biggest gainers look impressive even when compared with the powerful market rally stocks experienced
after bottoming in early March. That surge carried the Dow Jones Industrial Average up 59.3% and the
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index up 64.8% from their March 9 lows, and up 18.8% and 23.5%, respectively,
for the year.
Still, the sharp rally off the March lows wasn't enough to pull some large stocks into positive territory for the
year. Bailed-out banks contending with government involvement were among the biggest losers among
large-cap issues.
Despite recent moves by Citigroup to repay its government aid and exit from the TARP federal bailout
program, its shares finished the year at $3.31, down 50.7%. The Dow Jones banking-sector index was
among the worst performers in 2009, down 4.6%.
Citi's performance placed it in a tie for the worst-performing large-cap stock of 2009 with Rostelecom. The
Russian telecommunications firm also tumbled 50.7%, amid concerns about the government's planned
reorganization of the sector.
American depositary shares of Lloyds Banking Group fell 40.8% for the year. The U.K. bank has been partly
owned by the British government since it ran into difficulties after its acquisition of U.K. lender HBOS.
Some of the biggest gainers rose as investors positioned themselves for a recovery in the global economy,
and took defensive positions against a U.S. dollar that largely weakened throughout the year, as well as the
possibility of inflation.
In addition to Freeport-McMoRan, Anglo-Australian mining giant Rio Tinto also ranked among the top
performers for the year. Its ADSs nearly tripled.
Bullish technology investors also often opted for foreign companies, in light of some forecasts for sluggish
U.S. growth coming out of the recession. "The rate of change is going to be greater in emerging markets and
in commodity-based markets," said Stephen Wood, chief market strategist for Russell Investments.
ADSs of Indian outsourcing company Wipro more than doubled. The run-up reflects not only a focus on
corporate cost cutting, but also the expectation that growth in emerging markets may outpace the recovery
among developed countries.

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some U.S. tech firms also rebounded over the course of the year. Shares of Cognizant Technology Solutions,
a provider of information technology, consulting and business-process outsourcing, saw shares rise 151%.
Shares of tech firms fall under the broad theme of efficiency-seeking among corporations. Technology also
boasts an added benefit for investors who may still be rattled by the solvency issues seen by many
companies during the financial crisis.
Tech companies "have the best balance sheets of any sector," says Burt White, chief investment officer at
LPL Financial, and offer investors stability as well as upside opportunity as the economy recovers. "I can't
think of any other sector that does both," Mr. White said.
Amazon.com rose 162.3%, as investors became more optimistic that consumers would again start spending
and about the prospects for the Kindle e-reader. Similar expectations about consumers seemed to buoy
Apple, which rose 146.9% after being weighed down in early 2009 by bleak forecasts for consumer spending.
Among the fastest rising small and midcap companies, some did even better. Human Genome Sciences
benefited from good test results for its experimental Lupus drug Benlysta, which the company is developing
with GlaxoSmithKline. Human Genome Sciences leapt 1,342.5%, to $30.58. The stock ended 2008 at $2.12.
But a smaller pharmaceutical company was one of the largest decliners among small and midcap stocks.
Repros Therapeutics fell 92.4%, as it ran into snags in development of its Proellex treatment for uterine
fibroids and endometriosis.
The worst-performing small and midcap stock was RHI Entertainment, a maker of made-for-TV movies. Its
shares fell 96.2%, amid weak prices and volumes for its movies, miniseries and other TV programs as well as
concerns about debt.
Some smaller financial firms also fared poorly in 2009. Despite receiving $180.6 million from the U.S.
Treasury in November 2008 as part of the Troubled Asset Relief Program, shares of Pacific Capital Bancorp,
a Santa Barbara, Calif., lender, continued to slide at the start of 2009. After stabilizing slightly, the decline
picked up speed in June around the bank's announcement that it suspended dividend payments to the
government. Pacific Capital Bancorp finished the year down 94.3%.
Government involvement did help stabilize some companies. Federal aid to General Motors and Chrysler, for
example, helped shore up not only those companies, but also many auto suppliers that also had been beaten
down severely. Parts maker TRW Automotive Holdings rose more than six-fold as the prospects for the auto
sector recovered somewhat.
After the stomach-churning dive and rebound of auto makers and parts companies in 2009, analysts say
sturdier sectors such as technology, basic materials, industrials, financials and consumer discretionaries
should do well in 2010, so long as the global economy continues to heal, they say. "The sectors that have
been leading the pack, for the most part, are going to continue to lead," said Robert Pavlik, chief market
strategist for Banyan Partners LLC.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- Up-and-Down 2009 for Dollar --- Risk Aversion, Then Appetite Drove a Broad Swing; Can Year-End...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- Up-and-Down 2009 for Dollar --- Risk Aversion, Then
Appetite Drove a Broad Swing; Can Year-End Rally Go On?
By Joanna Slater
1,039 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After powering through the financial crisis, the U.S. dollar flagged in 2009 as investors rediscovered their
taste for risk and refocused on the greenback's flaws.
An index from J.P. Morgan Chase that tracks the buck against a basket of 16 currencies finished the year
down 5%. That figure masks a considerable swing: At one point in the spring, the index was up as much as
8%, while in the latter part of the year, it had switched to being down by the same percentage.
The dollar staged a short but vigorous rally as the year drew to a close: Economic data out of the U.S.
surpassed expectations while countries like Greece and Spain -- both users of the euro -- suddenly appeared
less creditworthy than before.
For some investors, the move raised the tantalizing prospect that a bigger change is under way for the dollar.
Rather than trading as a proxy for how much risk investors are willing to take -- as it has throughout the
financial crisis -- the buck may once again rise and fall on the state of the U.S. economy as it compares to its
global peers.
Investors are likely to focus on relative economic performance, says Jeremy Urwin, a currency trader at
Barclays Capital in New York, instead of simple "risk-on, risk-off" bets. In particular, differences in interest
rates between countries "are going to come to the fore" for currencies in 2010, he says.
That could be good news for the dollar, if the U.S. economy continues to exceed expectations while others
disappoint. But if the recovery proves more fragile, and if the efforts to fight the financial crisis end up spurring
unhealthy inflation, the dollar could stagger backward.
Early in 2009, the dollar appeared unstoppable. The financial system continued to totter, stocks were
plunging, and bad economic news was unabated. Investors flocked to the dollar, as they had throughout the
crisis, as a possible harbor in a storm.
By April, however, the strenuous efforts by central banks to stem the damage had begun to work. And as
investors began to put their cash to work again, some began shunning dollars.
The dollar's slide accelerated in the fall, when the U.S. appeared to be limping toward recovery, as other
economies walked, or even ran. Australia and Norway, for instance, repeatedly raised interest rates to rein
in growth while in the U.S., the Federal Reserve remained firmly on hold at near-zero levels.
The spigot of cheap money from the Fed, aimed at spurring economic activity, encouraged investors to
borrow dollars and use them to buy everything from other currencies to stocks to gold to commodities.
The maneuver is broadly known as a "carry trade," or borrowing funds at exceedingly low rates and investing
them to earn higher rates of return (or "carry").
Such trades are sensitive to changes in investors' appetite for risk, since volatile market moves can render
them unprofitable. When investors get nervous, they often buy back the currency in which they borrowed to
fund the carry trade.
For much of the fall, financial markets appeared fixed on a single relationship: the dollar versus everything
else. When the dollar weakened, stocks, oil, and gold rose, and vice-versa.
The inverse link between the dollar's path and the movement of the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index
touched a historical extreme in early December, according to a report from Deutsche Bank. The correlation
between the two -- a measure of how much they move together, or in opposite directions -- was the most
negative in more than 30 years, the report said.

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
December saw an abrupt change. Data on U.S. employment and retail sales provided positive surprises,
while investors heard a series of gloomy predictions from credit-rating firms about unsustainable deficits in
Greece, whose rating suffered. Adding to the mix: scant year-end trading, which tends to exacerbate price
swings. The euro fell almost 6% against the dollar in three weeks.
"It is very tempting to conclude that the world has changed and that the dollar is heading for longer-term
gains," Jens Nordvig, a currency strategist at Nomura Securities, said in a recent note. "But the
[macroeconomic] news flow has been much less dramatic than the price action." Mr. Nordvig recommended
bets against the dollar.
Late in New York on Dec. 31, one euro bought $1.4316, a decline of 2.4% in the value of the dollar from a
year earlier. However, the dollar managed to squeak out an annual gain versus the yen of 2.6%, fetching
93.08 yen at year's end. Currencies beaten down in the financial crisis like the Australian dollar and Brazilian
real came roaring back to life, surging 26% and 33%, respectively, versus the greenback.
At various points during the year, the dollar's status as the world's reserve currency came under scrutiny. It hit
headlines in March, when, in an unusual move, China's central bank governor publicly questioned the
desirability of a single reserve currency, and suggested a greater role for a little-used artificial currency
created by the International Monetary Fund. Leaders in Russia and Brazil also expressed concerns about the
dollar's primacy.
However, few believe the dollar's status as the dominant reserve currency is in jeopardy anytime soon, in part
because of a lack of alternatives. The euro's own weaknesses have been on full display in recent months,
given the widely divergent economic paths of countries like Greece and Germany, which share the currency.
Some say the Chinese yuan could assume a larger role in global finance, but that remains many years away.
Indeed, the Chinese government hewed to its longstanding policy of linking the yuan to the U.S. dollar.
Rather than permit some gradual strengthening, as they did in 2008, Chinese authorities held the yuan
steady against the dollar throughout 2009. That irked their neighbors, since it meant the yuan effectively
weakened along with the dollar late in the year, rendering Chinese exports more competitive abroad.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- World Stocks Return to Pre-Crisis Form --- After Best Rally in 2000s, European Shares Are Seen...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- World Stocks Return to Pre-Crisis Form --- After Best
Rally in 2000s, European Shares Are Seen Hitting Some Bumps in '10
By Neil Shah
1,216 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R3
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After staging the strongest rally in a decade, European stocks are expected to start losing steam at some
point in 2010.
Investors gobbled up European stocks in 2009. The pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx index of Europe's 600
biggest companies rose 28.6% to 253.16, its biggest annual percentage gain since 1999, when it gained
35.9%. (This index lost 46% in 2008, however.)
Individual markets also roared, in many cases returning to levels seen as the financial crisis snowballed in the
autumn of 2008. Germany's DAX index gained 23.8% to 5957.43, just shy of a 15-month high, while Britain's
blue-chip FTSE 100 index rose 22.1% to 5412.88, its biggest one-year percentage gain in a dozen years.
Many markets farther east fared even better. Hungary's main market soared 73.4% to 21227.01, while
Russia's RTS Index was the region's best performer, rising 128.6% to 1444.61.
Gradually recovering economies and healthier corporate profits are expected to keep investors interested in
stocks for much of this year. But the investment climate will get trickier as the world's major central banks look
to start unwinding crisis-era measures that flooded the financial system with money. Also, more attention will
be paid to governments struggling with heavy debts, such as Greece, Spain and the U.K.
"It's hard to see things going smoothly without any hiccups," says Daniele Scilingo, head of European stocks
at Pictet Asset Management in London, which manages 1.5 billion euros ($2.15 billion) of European stocks
for institutional clients. "What we see is uncertainty, question marks and volatility." He expects European
shares to hit major turbulence but still finish the year a little higher.
Last year's gains look even larger when compared with the lows reached in early March. Since March 9, the
pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx 600 index soared 60.3%. A big reason: easy-money policies by central
banks. Super-low interest rates have encouraged investors to move out of savings accounts and into
higher-yielding investments like European and emerging-market stocks.
Europe's banks were among the big gainers in 2009 after governments stepped in to rescue the financial
system. In the U.K., Barclays PLC leapt 79.9% for the year, while Switzerland's UBS AG, one of the biggest
casualties of the financial crisis, increased 87.3% from its March 9 low.
Banking stocks could come under pressure this year as governments withdraw special measures that helped
the banks fund themselves cheaply, analysts warn. Global regulators could also move to tighten requirements
on the amount of extra capital banks must hold in reserve, hitting their profitability.
More broadly, investor worries have started shifting from banks and the economy to the governments that
came to the rescue last year. Concern about the public finances of several countries has intensified in recent
months as investors question whether these economies will buckle beneath the weight of heavy debts and
budget deficits. Greece's deficit was forecast to hit 12.7% of its economic output in 2009, while the U.K. also
faces a deficit north of 12%.
Illustrating the rising worry, investors are demanding a higher yield premium to hold Greek and U.K.
government bonds compared with German government debt, which is considered the European gold
standard. To restore investor confidence, these countries will have to raise taxes and cut public spending, a
combination that could hurt their fragile economies.
In Britain, such efforts may be complicated by elections that must be held before June. Some expect that
Britain's opposition party, the Conservatives, will beat Prime Minister Gordon Brown's governing Labour Party
but fail to secure enough support in Parliament to pass major fiscal reforms. That could place Britain's triple-A
credit rating in jeopardy. A credit downgrade would increase Britain's borrowing costs at a time when the
government needs to borrow a lot of money.

--- Page 173 ---
Page 173 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Another major hurdle: Central banks, including those in Europe, are starting to think about raising short-term
interest rates from record-low levels. The European Central Bank, which handles monetary policy for the
16 nations that share the euro currency, faces a particularly tough bind: If it moves too fast to normalize
interest rates, it could snuff out nascent recoveries in weaker euro economies like Greece. However, if the
ECB moves too slowly, it could stoke inflation in stronger economies like Germany.
While there has been debate over when central banks will raise rates -- some don't expect increases until
2011 -- many analysts see the ECB, the U.K.'s Bank of England and the U.S. Federal Reserve at least
starting to end emergency-support programs by roughly midyear.
Lurking in the background is the fact that investors still aren't confident that Europe's economy is out of the
woods. Even stronger countries like France and Germany could suffer a relapse into recession as
governments and central banks take stimulus measures away.
To be sure, corporate Europe has weathered the crisis surprisingly well. In early 2009, investors feared a
catastrophic wave of European corporate defaults and bankruptcies that never fully materialized, thanks to
the ability of many larger companies to refinance their loans by selling corporate bonds. And analysts at J.P.
Morgan Chase &Co., Deutsche Bank AG and Credit Suisse Group AG all expect European stocks to notch
further gains this year as readings of the economy improve and investors put fresh cash to work in an
environment of tame inflation.
James Bevan, chief investment officer at CCLA Investment Management in London, says shares in
Continental Europe can fare well even when markets are expecting higher interest rates, thanks to the
region's relatively low debt levels. He particularly likes Germany, which tends to be sensitive to global
manufacturing and trade trends.
But like many bank strategists, Mr. Bevan is worried about midyear wobbling caused by central banks and
potential problems in the bond-market. He says European stocks may gain but then finish the year lower, with
Britain's FTSE 100 index ending at 5000 after hitting 5750.
"The second half of the year is going to be more difficult," he says. "We just haven't gotten much evidence
that the economy can sustain itself going into the next [interest-rate] tightening cycle."
Analysts at Morgan Stanley, meanwhile, are gloomier: They see European stocks losing about 5% in 2010.
An additional concern is that European stocks mightn't be all that cheap anymore. Pictet Asset Management's
Mr. Scilingo says that European shares are trading around 18 times expected 2009 earnings, or higher than
their recent average. "We are a touch above where we should be," he says.
As such, he says, this "will be the year to discriminate between what you want to hold, and what's gone up
because of the market euphoria."

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- Metals Have the Mettle to Rally On --- All That Glitters Is Not Gold, as Base Metals Had 2009's...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- Metals Have the Mettle to Rally On --- All That Glitters
Is Not Gold, as Base Metals Had 2009's Best Gains; Demand Stands to Rise in 2010
By Carolyn Cui
1,026 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R5
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Metals finished the year as the best-performing commodities, riding on hopes for a global economic recovery
and fears of runaway inflation.
The rally is likely to extend into the new year, as demand from emerging economies remains strong while
developed nations are catching up. But it could be capped if central banks slam the brakes on stimulus
spending and raise interest rates.
While commodity prices rebounded sharply from lows early in the year, most closed well below records set
before the economic crisis began in 2008, underpinning the reality that the recovery has just begun to gather
momentum. The Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index added nearly 19% to 117.24 in 2009, about 41% off its
peak in 2008.
Leading the way in 2009 were base metals. Copper, which has many industrial uses, more than doubled,
soaring 139%. It ended the year at $3.3275 per pound but still 18% shy of record prices hit in 2008. Lead,
which is used in car batteries, also more than doubled, to $2,416 a metric ton, followed by zinc, which was up
125%, and aluminum, gaining 50%.
Precious metals reaped smaller gains but stole the limelight. Gold was up 24% for the year to $1,095.20 per
troy ounce at Comex, the metals division of CME Group. It set 27 exchange records in the past 12 months
amid waves of buying from funds and individual investors seeking a haven against inflation and a dropping
dollar. But it is still 52% below its inflation-adjusted high of $2,300 dating from 1980. Silver surged 49% to
$16.822 per troy ounce.
Platinum and palladium -- primarily used by auto makers in catalytic converters -- jumped 56% and 117%,
respectively. Their gains came on the prospect that car makers will sharply increase their production in 2010,
as U.S. auto sales improved in recent months, along with record sales in emerging markets such as China
and Brazil.
Overall, base metals were fueled particularly by Chinese demand, which comprises 43% of the world's total
and gained 27% from a year earlier, according to Deutsche Bank. Take China out of the equation, and global
metals demand fell 9% in 2009.
"In 2009, the big surprise to me was the degree to which China has recovered," said Theresa Gusman, head
of global commodities at DB Advisors, an asset-management subsidiary of Deutsche Bank.
The outlook for individual base metals will depend on how quickly producers can respond to rising demand.
Demand for base metals is likely to get further support as Western manufacturers start to rebuild their
inventories next year. Many companies are running low on their stocks of raw materials, as warehouses are
filled with unsold finished goods and credit remains tight, Ms. Gusman said.
For 2010, "robust economic growth in the emerging markets continues to be an ever greater pull on the
world's resources," Goldman Sachs Group analysts wrote in a research note. Goldman expects a small deficit
to emerge in the global copper market in 2010, mainly due to robust demand growth from power and
construction sectors in China and India.
Copper remains "our most favored metal," as demand growth will outstrip supply, said Goldman analysts,
who predict prices could gain 10% over the next six months.
However, they added, aluminum and zinc are likely to see a glut because of years of excessive capacity
expansions, in spite of a budding recovery in the U.S. construction market. Goldman expects prices of
aluminum and zinc to retreat 4% and 8%, respectively, over the next three months.

--- Page 176 ---
Page 176 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold was the hottest commodity in 2009. Central banks in India and Russia bought hundreds of tons of gold
to diversify their foreign reserves, making central banks, as a whole, a net buyer of gold in 2009 after
decades of selling.
Retail, or individual, investors around the world stocked up on gold bullion and flocked into gold-backed
exchange-traded funds to preserve their net worth. SPDR Gold Shares, the world's biggest gold ETF,
increased its gold holdings by 45%, to more than 1,133 metric tons, valued at $40.2 billion at current market
prices.
The precious metal, which like other commodities is traded in dollar terms, reflected the decline in the U.S.
currency earlier in the year. Gold prices retreated as the dollar rose late in the year. The gold frenzy also
abated as some gold bulls decided to take profits, leading to a 10% retreat in prices in December. Silver lost
13% at the same time.
Many analysts remain bullish on gold in the near term. J.P. Morgan Chase analyst Michael Jansen expects
the price of gold to reach $1,400 by mid-2010, as long as "the liquidity spigot remains open and the U.S.
dollar remains under pressure." Goldman analysts set a six-month price target of $1,260 for gold, citing the
favorable low interest-rate environment.
"Next year, we will see new highs in gold without any doubt," mainly driven by renewed pressure on the
dollar, as central banks in Europe and China may raise interest rates sooner than the Federal Reserve in the
U.S., said Philip Klapwijk, chairman of GFMS Ltd., a London-based metals consultancy. A double-digit
jobless rate in the U.S. is likely to restrain the Fed, Mr. Klapwijk said.
Still, the forces driving the gold rally are temporary, such as fiscal stimulus packages and central banks'
easy-money policies. In 2010, analysts say, the primary risk for gold is an earlier-than-expected increase in
interest rates, especially in the U.S., if the economy strengthens. In early 1980s, a drastic tightening by the
Fed sent gold into a decade-long bear market.
Mr. Jansen of J.P. Morgan expects gold to peak after the second quarter of 2010, as "reduced expectations
around inflation, higher global interest rates and a mildly stronger" dollar will push gold prices lower.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- For Rebuilt Markets, a Test in 2010 --- Government Saved the Financial System From Collapse...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- For Rebuilt Markets, a Test in 2010 --- Government
Saved the Financial System From Collapse. Now Investors Have to Stand on Their Own
By Tom Lauricella
1,254 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Financial markets staged a remarkable recovery in 2009, coming back from the brink of disaster thanks to
unprecedented rescue efforts by governments around the globe.
The question now facing investors is how the markets will stand on their own in 2010.
After tumbling 25.4% to a 12-year low in March, the Dow Jones Industrial Average staged a blistering
rebound to finish the year up 18.8% at 10428.05. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 23.5% to
1115.10 in 2009.
It wasn't just big stocks that rallied. Encouraged by government efforts to restart the financial system and
stimulate the economy, investors who had fled from all but the very safest investments in 2008 dove back into
the most volatile stocks and bonds, racking up big gains along the way.
The Dow's low point on March 9, when it was down 53.8% from its all-time high in October 2007, turned out
to be the start of a powerful rally. Although the gains slowed in December and the Dow slid 120 points on the
last day of the year, it finished 2009 up 59.3% from its March low, the fastest climb since 1933. The Nasdaq
Composite ended 2009 78.9% above its March low and up 43.9% for the year.
Companies in sectors that had taken the biggest beating in 2008 and early last year surged, including autos
and basic materials. Ford Motor shares more than quadrupled to $10, while Freeport-McMoRan Copper
&Gold more than tripled.
"You had an unprecedented amount of stimulus -- both fiscal and monetary . . . and you had unprecedented
degrees of skepticism and pessimism," says Scott Migliori, chief investment officer for RCM Capital
Management in San Francisco.
Going into the new year, the economy seems to be showing enough momentum that few investors fear a
sudden dip back into recession, although most still expect a slow recovery. Corporate earnings, which have
been far stronger than expected thanks to aggressive cost-cutting, seem on track to close out 2009 with
another solid reading.
The market continues to benefit from the Federal Reserve's pledge to keep interest rates low for an
"extended period" and the federal government's stimulus dollars that continue to pour into the economy.
Stock-market valuations, while not cheap, aren't so rich that investors see better opportunity in other assets. If
anything, many investors are more wary of a meaningful pullback in corporate-bond prices after a big rally in
2009.
Although many analysts expect the stock market to end 2010 with gains, they see some bumps along the
way. A major unknown is how the markets will fare once the Fed starts siphoning off some of the
unprecedented amounts of cash it pumped into the financial markets to the support the economy, especially if
the Fed moves sooner rather than later.
Investors will pay close attention to hints from the Fed as to how it's going proceed, and watching for any
signs that the central bank's exit strategy is proving disruptive. They especially will watch the housing
market, which has been propped up by the Fed's successful effort to keep mortgage rates low through
purchases of mortgage-backed securities.
In the past, the onset of interest-rate increases "at the very least led to some consolidation and indigestion,"
says Ronan Carr, European equity strategist at Morgan Stanley. Although he predicts a "strong year" for
stocks, he adds that, "our fear is that you do have a negative effect on growth in 2011 on the back of tighter
monetary policy."

--- Page 179 ---
Page 179 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors could decide the Fed is risking an inflation flare-up if it keeps rates too low. That could lead to a
rise in long-term interest rates that could hurt the housing market.
Also hovering over the markets is a fear that the massive U.S. government deficit will lead to a steep decline
in the dollar.
"The challenge going into [2010] is the costs associates with massive deficit spending and keeping interest
rates at zero for an extended period of time," says Jason DeSena Trennert, chief investment strategist at
Strategas Research Partners.
While many companies posted big stock gains in 2009 simply because it turned out they weren't going out of
business, investors this year will be more discriminating, Mr. Trennert says. Companies "aren't going to get
extra credit for just surviving; you're actually going to have to perform."
Looking back on the past year, Mr. Trennert says, "the good news is we're still standing and we've survived."
As good as 2009 was, the Dow remains down 26.4% from its all time high of 14164.53, and more
discouraging, is down 9.3% from 10 years ago. For the Dow, which began in 1896 and now has 30
components, it was the worst decade since the 1930s, when it dropped 39.5%.
Measured more broadly, stocks traded on the New York Stock Exchange have lost an average of 0.32% per
year, including dividends, since the end of 1999. That makes it the worst calendar decade for stocks going all
the way back to the 1820s, when reliable stock market records begin, according to data compiled by Yale
University finance professor William Goetzmann.
Many individual investors remained skeptical. Between March and the end of November, they pulled $13
billion out of U.S. stock mutual funds and poured $239 billion into bond funds, according to Morningstar Inc.
Although some of the money heading into bond funds reflected a shift out of money-market funds yielding
essentially zero, as recently as November, investors were still exiting from U.S stock funds, withdrawing $7.7
billion that month.
"We're probably still only in the early stages of a potentially big economic recovery but I think it's probably not
until well into next year that investors begin to broadly recognize it," says Thomas Lee, U.S. equity strategist
at J.P. Morgan Chase.
Mr. Lee thinks there's enough slack in the job market to keep inflation from becoming an near-term threat as
companies begin hiring. That, in turn, should allow the Fed to keep interest rates low for a prolonged period
of time, he says.
RCM's Mr. Migliori also thinks 2010 will get off to a good start. Many of the trends that helped stocks rebound
"will carry forward at least through the first and second quarters," he says. However, "as you get into the latter
stages of 2010, he says, "a lot of the tail winds from monetary and fiscal stimulus are likely to become more
headwinds."
Mr. Migliori thinks the market is especially vulnerable to the risk of the Fed raising rates, even if it's just by a
half or three-quarters of percentage point. "Given the fact that stocks have essentially gone straight up from
March 9, it could be enough to cause a decent-sized correction as folks recalibrate their expectations for Fed
actions," he says.
Ultimately, however, he expects a decent year for stocks as the economic recovery takes hold. Improving
fundamentals "should be enough to offset a modest rise in rates," he says, especially among technology and
energy stocks.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- For Rebuilt Markets, a Test in 2010 --- Much Talk, But Little Changed on Wall Street
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2009 --- For Rebuilt Markets, a Test in 2010 --- Much Talk, But
Little Changed on Wall Street
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,384 words
4 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
So much has changed over the past year.
Or has it?
On the heels of the most harrowing period for financial markets since the Great Depression, politicians,
regulators, investors and Wall Street executives shared a vow: never again. They would reform the system
and change behavior to prevent another financial calamity.
Some significant changes have been made, such as slashing Wall Street's debt, scrutinizing compensation,
simplifying financial products and potentially increasing surveillance of financial markets.
But more than a year after Lehman Brothers, American International Group Inc., Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac
and Washington Mutual collapsed or were saved by the government and financial markets swooned, it is
striking how little on Wall Street has changed.
Indeed, the surge in markets around the globe last year suggests that fear has been replaced by relative
complacency.
"We've been successful in identifying, appreciating, and debating the big systemic issues," says Daniel
Alpert, managing partner of Westwood Capital LLC, a New York investment bank. "But we've done precious
little to effect measures that reliably protect the future of our global financial system."
That banks and Wall Street firms have slashed their heavy debt is a positive step since it was this heavy
borrowing that put them in a precarious position. Most major investments banks now borrow money at about
half 2007 levels, according to research firm CreditSights.
These levels are "more manageable and don't pose inordinate systemic risk," says David Hendler, a
CreditSights analyst. "We won't go back to [previous levels] for years."
During the two decades leading up to the financial crisis, financial products became increasingly complex.
Collateralized debt obligations, or CDOs, begat "synthetic CDOs" which begat "CDO-squareds." Eventually,
senior executives didn't understand products their own firms had created, and the firms held too many of
these products for themselves. Lately, however, financial firms are shying away from churning out
complicated, newfangled products, potentially reducing the likelihood that a big firm will be caught with
unexpected losses.
A year after Bernard Madoff acknowledged the biggest Ponzi scheme in history, a scam that regulators
missed for years, there are signs they are stepping up their scrutiny. Hedge fund Galleon Group is being
investigated for alleged insider trading, for example, and some recent reforms have been unveiled at the
Securities and Exchange Commission. For example, the SEC now is scrutinizing flows of information on Wall
Street -- where traders, bankers, lawyers and other players swap information and favors.
Meanwhile, changes in the way Wall Street's employees are paid may reduce the incentive to embrace risky
short-term moves. Bonuses for 2009 that will be paid to the top 30 Goldman Sachs Group Inc. executives, for
example, will be in stock that can't be sold for five years, rather than cash. (Most employees the firm will still
have cash as a component of their bonuses, however.) At Citigroup Inc. and other firms, base salaries have
been raised, and bonuses cut.
Employees of smaller banks still benefiting from the government's Troubled Asset Relief Program face other
curbs on pay, and the U.K. has instituted even stricter measures, though many larger banks have handed
TARP money back to the government, allowing them to hand out big bonuses if they choose.

--- Page 181 ---
Page 181 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But some suspect it will be business as usual once the government money is paid back. Wall Street firms still
generally pay more than half their revenue to employees as compensation, a figure that actually climbed over
the past year. Despite predictions that the financial sector would shrink dramatically, allowing brain power to
be employed in other fields, the head count in the financial industry is down just 10% in the past year,
according to the Securities Industry and Financial Markets Association.
And while firms have cut their debt, some hedge funds, like Paulson &Co., are boosting their leverage. With
interest rates at superlow levels, other financial firms could be tempted to increase their own borrowings.
Indeed, even amid all the change, much more has stayed the same. Credit-rating companies, excoriated for
placing top grades on what turned out to be toxic debt, still face the same potential conflicts of interest they
always faced: These companies are paid by the very parties churning out the debt that is being rated.
At the same time, trading accounts for more than half the recent revenue of Wall Street firms, according to
analysts. This suggests a continued dependence on volatile investment profits even as some firms say they
are doing less trading for their own accounts, and more for clients, a potentially less risky endeavor.
Meanwhile, there is little chatter about breaking up huge firms like J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. or Goldman
Sachs, even though a clear lesson of 2008's collapse is that firms can grow so large that their troubles cause
problems for the overall economy.
Just as important, there have been few big changes in markets for derivative securities, such as credit-default
swaps. One proposal aired -- to force trading onto public exchanges where it can be better monitored -- has
yet to be implemented, even though some argue that the inability to fully monitor exposure to these
derivatives among companies like American International Group paved the way for financial meltdown in
2008.
Nor have rules been changed to increase the amount of capital that U.S. banks must hold as a percentage of
assets, despite a range of recent proposals. Although many banks have boosted the capital they hold, without
regulation the temptation will remain to eventually reduce these reserves.
"Until that's resolved, there's really no safety," says Westwood Capital's Mr. Alpert.
Meanwhile, there are new signs of potential financial bubbles, as investors take advantage of ultra-low
interest rates to borrow money and chase rising investments. Stocks in some emerging-market nations,
such as Argentina and Russia were up as much as 100% in 2009, while some real-estate markets in Asia are
surging anew. Some bubble-watchers also point to gold prices, which surged to more than $1,217 an ounce
in the fall, from $883 at the start of 2009, and ended the year around $1,100.
"China is going through another boom in housing prices, as well as in stock prices," notes Robert Shiller, the
Yale University economist who warned about the technology and housing bubbles. "We have seen
real-estate prices rising fast in some cities, notably San Francisco and Minneapolis."
At its heart, the economic downturn was caused by home buyers and financial firms piling on too much debt,
and by borrowing at rates low enough to spark ever-more speculation.
Today, debt is largely a government issue, as if the problem has been shifted from the private sector to the
public one, like a game of kick-the-can. Just as overleveraged consumers and home buyers vowed to trim
debt down the line, federal and local officials provide assurances that they will eventually do something about
their own borrowing, even as they tackle potentially costly new initiatives, including the military surge in
Afghanistan, health-care reform, and initiatives to tackle climate change.
Likewise, the Federal Reserve is confident it can raise interest rates before new troubles brew. But the Fed's
track record in recent years suggests it will take its time. Scrutiny by Congress could make it more difficult to
increase rates.
While some economists predict taxes will have to rise to chip away at some of the nation's debt, politicians of
both parties have demonstrated little willingness to consider higher levies.
Just as home buyers figured rising real estate would let them handle their debt, some nations are counting on
economic growth to improve the health of their balance sheets. If there is a strong economic recovery, the
Fed may be able to slowly raise rates -- perhaps starting this year -- without causing much damage. Over the
long haul, though, all the government borrowing likely will put pressure on interest rates and represent a
headwind for markets and the global economy.
(See related article: "Government Saved the Financial System From Collapse. Now Investors Have to Stand
on Their Own" -- WSJ Jan. 4, 2010)

The True Causes of the Economic Meltdown of 2008
Letters to the Editor
The True Causes of the Economic Meltdown of 2008
707 words
2 January 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Zachary Karabell rightly decries the development of a false thesis concerning the need for a "rebalancing" of
global economic activity ("The 'Global Imbalances' Myth," op-ed, Dec. 21). With respect to the origins of the
current downturn, however, he has joined the Obama administration in buying into the false conventional
wisdom that our recent crisis was "triggered by massive misplaced bets on the U.S. housing market and
trillions of dollars of [innovative] derivatives" based on such malinvestment; as well as "electronic and
instantaneous" capital flows; "greed on the part of banks and investors world-wide"; and an "economic fusion
between China and the U.S. that kept interest rates low and inflation lower."
Not once does Mr. Karabell mention the core root of all of these recent phenomena: the Federal Reserve's
extreme monetary ease for most of this decade. Loose money for a long period falsified the term structure of
interest rates, leading to distorted patterns of capital investment and resource allocation. Further, asset
values became distorted across the globe, as other nations acted in concert with the Fed, multiplying global
investment and trade errors and imbalances.
This continued monetary pumping also led to the development of complex hedging instruments in the form of
derivative securities, as investors sought to protect themselves from asset values changing primarily due to
volatility in monetary relations. Increasing amounts of credit in the banking system always generate higher
leverage on corporate balance sheets, thus intensifying the eventual correction.
The inability of Mr. Karabell and President Obama to apprehend the origins of the "Great Recession" of 2008
in turn leads to convoluted policy recommendations. Mr. Karabell states, for example, that increased savings
in the U.S., an unambiguously good thing, might nonetheless lead to "underinvestment in innovation,
education and infrastructure." Since when did increased saving lead to underinvestment?
But Mr. Obama agrees, saying we will "spend our way out of this recession." Mr. Karabell further states that
China should continue its state spending binge so as not to disrupt domestic growth in China or global supply
chains; one can only wonder what he thought of the inverse relationship between state spending and
economic growth in, say, Japan, post-1990.
Mark S. Wilser
Irvine, Calif.
---
Mr. Karabell misses an opportunity to explain the true cause of our most recent crisis: The real reason for our
crisis is that the world lacks a true anchor of value. A system designed around an anchor of value features
natural self-correcting mechanisms. This currently does not exist.
Mr. Karabell says, "The blunt fact is that at no point in the past century has there been anything resembling a
global economic equilibrium." In fact, the U.S. current account balance spent decades hovering around zero
(i.e., balanced) prior to turning sharply negative in the early 1980s.
Earlier in the century, while on the gold standard, a large current account deficit would be met with a large
outflow of gold, which would act as a tightening on the banking system and would have kept unrestrained
growth in credit and consumer spending in check. It would not have been possible to run the current account
deficit the U.S. ran in the past 20 years if there were an anchor of value. Without the unrestrained credit
growth that the U.S. experienced, the crisis would not have happened.
The values of the stock market, corporate and government debt markets, commodities and currencies
fluctuate with the sentiment of market participants whose history with regards to stability is bipolar at best.
The resulting high volatility in oil price, its impact on our current account, the dollar and credit markets all

--- Page 184 ---
Page 184 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
emanate from this unanchored system. This volatility can serve as a trigger to financial instability when the
underlying imbalances get precarious enough.
To be clear, a system with a global anchor does not mean that there will not be high volatility. When we were
on the gold standard we experienced many booms and busts but arguably they were kept in check since the
underlying imbalances were never allowed to get so large as to threaten the whole system.
Kenneth Bauso, C.F.A.
New York

Sayonara to the Rabbit Hutch: Living With Roommates in Japan --- Big Vacant Apartments Make Perfect Digs For Modern Women on a Budget
Sayonara to the Rabbit Hutch: Living With Roommates in Japan --- Big Vacant Apartments Make
Perfect Digs For Modern Women on a Budget
By Mariko Sanchanta
1,205 words
30 December 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO -- Olivia Burrell, a 32-year-old Canadian gospel singer, was fed up with living in Lilliputian studio
apartments in Tokyo where she could see (and smell) her kitchen from her bed.
Three months ago, she took the plunge and moved in with five Japanese women living in a spacious
6-bedroom apartment in Harajuku, a buzzy neighborhood in the city center.
But, so far, her roommate experience hasn't quite been the Japanese version of "Friends" she had
envisioned. Ms. Burrell walked into the kitchen one evening to find no fewer than eight separate bottles of
dishwashing liquid on the kitchen counter, all neatly lined up and labeled with their owners' names.
"My roommates are neat and very courteous," says Ms. Burrell, who has lived in Japan for seven years and
who had lived with roommates in Canada. "But this whole concept is new here, and people don't naturally
want to share things as much."
Japan has no real tradition of roommates: People have preferred to live in their own tiny places. Now, fed up
with a dearth of reasonably priced apartments in desirable Tokyo neighborhoods, a growing number of
relatively affluent women in their 20s and 30s have started to create demand for a radical new segment of the
Japanese real-estate market: apartments to share.
The timing of the trend coincides with a glut of upscale apartments in Tokyo, which have flooded the market
since the collapse of Lehman Brothers last year. Foreign bankers decamped, leaving behind many three- and
four-bedroom apartments, popular with expatriates, which have been vacant for months.
Kumi Tahara, 27, and Kana Arai, 32, stepped into the void, founding a real-estate agency named "Tokyo
Girls' Real Estate." They persuaded some landlords to let them slice up four-bedroom apartments into as
many as 10 smaller rooms, which they then started renting out to young Japanese women. "After the Lehman
shock, a lot of gaijin [foreigners] left, and there are some places that have been vacant for more than half a
year," says Ms. Arai, who wears a pink sequined bow on her head and favors miniskirts and knee-high boots.
"The landlords pay the redesign fees and we're able to increase the rent."
One apartment in Roppongi, the expat mecca in Tokyo, commanded 450,000 yen a month (about $5,000)
before its previous tenants left. Ms. Tahara and Ms. Arai redesigned the place, chopping it up into eight small
rooms that are about 100 square feet each, which they rented for 80,000 yen apiece. The net result: They
increased rental income by 190,000 yen a month, of which they receive a 10% cut.
"The market is improving a little bit, but it's nowhere near as robust as it was before" Lehman's woes says
Keiko Matsumoto, a manager at Ken Real Estate Investment Advisors Ltd., an agent that caters to expats
and high net-worth Japanese. "I think the idea of facilitating shared housing is very smart in this
environment."
In Tokyo, one of the world's most densely populated cities, the concept of having roommates or sharing
quarters has never been popular. The majority of young Tokyoites choose to live in cramped, cluttered,
soulless studios known as usagi goya in Japanese, or "rabbit hutches."
It has been thought preferable to live in a shoebox than to share a space with strangers. The lack of
roommate-friendly apartments was also driven by developers who built up apartment complexes over the
decades that were often chopped up into tiny, single rooms to maximize rental income.
Ms. Tahara, a former Japan Airlines check-in attendant, and Ms. Arai met at a real-estate agency and took
note of the numerous requests for roommates they received from young, female clients. They sensed there
was a business idea in all of the requests and started their company two years ago. Since the inception of
Tokyo Girls' Real Estate, they have placed more than 100 women in 12 properties.

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Tahara says the company is profitable. Theirs is the only agency in Tokyo that caters exclusively to
working women in their 30s. Other "shared housing" businesses exist, but most handle old buildings in less
desirable locations that cater to backpackers, foreigners on a short stay or old people.
Central to the success of Ms. Arai and Ms. Tahara's business is that they redecorate and redesign interiors
themselves, adding touches such as claw-footed bathtubs, gold wallpaper, pink rhinestones and disco balls.
The uninspiring apartments end up as fantasy playlands. "I used to live in a shared home in Tokyo, but it was
so old and falling apart -- freezing in the winter, and sweltering in the summer," says Ms. Arai. "These
represent the dreams of these women, transformed into reality.We put lights on mirrors, so when they put on
makeup they feel like actresses."
But the newness of this roommate culture in Japan means that many young women aren't prepared for what
it's like to share housing. Ms. Arai and Ms. Tahara, who mediate disputes, say one of the biggest issues is
hair clogging up the shower drain.
And men aren't ever allowed inside the women's shared homes. Ms. Tahara and Ms. Arai believe that the
apartments should be a refuge for women. "If there was some sort of problem, then we couldn't be
responsible for it," says Ms. Tahara. "And if a guy ended up staying over a lot and used water and electricity,
it would create an unfair burden on the others."
Ms. Arai and Ms. Tahara interview all of the potential tenants before they sign a lease.
Demographic shifts in Japan have created a breed of single women who work late, are often out on the
weekends and just want a convenient place to crash. "The average age for marriage in Japan is steadily
increasing, and fewer females want to live at home with their parents," says Takanori Nakamura, a senior
research and development director at Hakuhodo Inc, a Japanese advertising agency. "Meanwhile, salaries
are decreasing and women want to maintain their lifestyles. Rent is the first thing they cut."
Each apartment that Ms. Arai and Ms. Tahara redecorate is done with a concept in mind. The theme of the
Harajuku apartment, where Ms. Burrell lives, is "Saturday Night Fever."
"The design here is very cute," says Sachiko Saito, a 28-year-old systems engineer who moved into the
apartment last week. "Japanese people aren't used to the idea of sharing an apartment, but I don't think it's
very difficult. I specifically looked for places on the Internet that I could share with others."
Ms. Burrell, who speaks Japanese, says she was bummed out at first about the "no men" rule. "I was a little
disappointed because I have a lot of male friends, but, ultimately, it makes sense. There are a lot of good
things about sharing. After living alone for so long here, I needed to be more conscious of people. I'll learn a
lot."

International Finance: Russia Sheds Debt Burden Of Soviet Era
International Finance: Russia Sheds Debt Burden Of Soviet Era
By Riva Froymovich
431 words
29 December 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Russia has rid itself of Soviet-era debt, paving the way for it to re-enter international capital markets after a
decade.
Russia exchanged $405.8 million of cash and sovereign Eurobonds maturing in 2010 and 2030 last week to
the remaining outstanding bondholders of Soviet debt.
"Completion of the last exchange represents the settlement of one of the most complex and comparatively
large categories of the external state Soviet-era debt," Russia's Finance Ministry said.
The government had said it intended to finish the exchange before issuing new bonds. The size of Russia's
Eurobond issue, seen early next year, could be up to $17.8 billion, according to Finance Minister Alexei
Kudrin, depending on the price of oil, the country's major export.
The nation's re-entry comes as fresh emerging-market sovereign debt is expected to reach critical mass.
Brazil, Venezuela, Mexico and Argentina are also forecast to sell new bonds in 2010, among others.
But demand isn't expected to be a problem for any of these issuers, many of which came to market en masse
in 2009 to feed funding requirements by taking advantage of heightened investor demand.
Russia's Finance Ministry has said it will lean more heavily on overseas debt markets through 2012 to finance
annual deficits, as Russia's oil wealth funds run down. Standard &Poor's said Monday that Russia's net
government borrowing requirement could come in at 6% of gross domestic product in 2010.
Last week, the ratings firm raised the sovereign outlook on Russia to stable from negative and affirmed its
rating at triple-B, noting that budget and balance-sheet performance will gradually improve. Mr. Kudrin said
the move created a "good environment" for the anticipated Eurobond offering.
"I think there will be high demand" for Russia's Eurobond, said John Chambers, chairman of Standard
&Poor's sovereign-rating committee. "It's a solid investment-grade credit."
Russia holds an investment grade rating from all three international ratings firms, due partially to its $440
billion gold and currency reserves, the world's third largest next to China and Japan. Mr. Chambers added
that Russia will be afforded more flexibility in financing by tapping international markets.
"Governments often come to market early to nail down their external borrowing needs. I think there will be
plenty of demand at all levels," Mr. Chambers said.
This year, investors sought to tap the higher yields of riskier credits while the developed world kept interest
rates near record lows.
